    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Whole-genome, transcriptome, and methylome analyses provide insights into the evolution of platycoside biosynthesis in Platycodon grandiflorus , a medicinal plant"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-07-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Triterpenoid saponins (TSs) are common plant defense phytochemicals with potential pharmaceutical properties. Platycodon grandiflorus (Campanulaceae) has been traditionally used to treat bronchitis and asthma in East Asia. The oleanane-type TSs, platycosides, are a major component of the P. grandiflorus root extract. Recent studies show that platycosides exhibit anti-inflammatory, antiobesity, anticancer, antiviral, and antiallergy properties. However, the evolutionary history of platycoside biosynthesis genes remains unknown. In this study, we sequenced the genome of P. grandiflorus and investigated the genes involved in platycoside biosynthesis. The draft genome of P. grandiflorus is 680.1&#8201;Mb long and contains 40,017 protein-coding genes. Genomic analysis revealed that the CYP716 family genes play a major role in platycoside oxidation. The CYP716 gene family of P. grandiflorus was much larger than that of other Asterid species. Orthologous gene annotation also revealed the expansion of &#946;-amyrin synthases (bASs) in P. grandiflorus, which was confirmed by tissue-specific gene expression. In these expanded gene families, we identified key genes showing preferential expression in roots and association with platycoside biosynthesis. In addition, whole-genome bisulfite sequencing showed that CYP716 and bAS genes are hypomethylated in P. grandiflorus, suggesting that epigenetic modification of these two gene families affects platycoside biosynthesis. Thus whole-genome, transcriptome, and methylome data of P. grandiflorus provide novel insights into the regulation of platycoside biosynthesis by CYP716 and bAS gene families."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Triterpenoid saponins (TSs) are common plant defense phytochemicals with potential pharmaceutical properties. Platycodon grandiflorus (Campanulaceae) has been traditionally used to treat bronchitis and asthma in East Asia. The oleanane-type TSs, platycosides, are a major component of the P. grandiflorus root extract. Recent studies show that platycosides exhibit anti-inflammatory, antiobesity, anticancer, antiviral, and antiallergy properties. However, the evolutionary history of platycoside biosynthesis genes remains unknown. The draft genome of P. grandiflorus is 680.1&#8201;Mb long and contains 40,017 protein-coding genes. Genomic analysis revealed that the CYP716 family genes play a major role in platycoside oxidation. The CYP716 gene family of P. grandiflorus was much larger than that of other Asterid species. Thus whole-genome, transcriptome, and methylome data of P. grandiflorus provide novel insights into the regulation of platycoside biosynthesis by CYP716 and bAS gene families."/>

                </header>

                
    <div class="js-hide" data-component="article-subject-links">
        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Subjects</h3>
        <ul class="c-article-subject-list">
            <li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/plant-molecular-biology" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Plant molecular biology</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/secondary-metabolism" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Secondary metabolism</a></li>
        </ul>

            </div>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Triterpenoid saponins (TSs) are common plant defense phytochemicals with potential pharmaceutical properties. <i>Platycodon grandiflorus</i> (Campanulaceae) has been traditionally used to treat bronchitis and asthma in East Asia. The oleanane-type TSs, platycosides, are a major component of the <i>P. grandiflorus</i> root extract. Recent studies show that platycosides exhibit anti-inflammatory, antiobesity, anticancer, antiviral, and antiallergy properties. However, the evolutionary history of platycoside biosynthesis genes remains unknown. In this study, we sequenced the genome of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> and investigated the genes involved in platycoside biosynthesis. The draft genome of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> is 680.1 Mb long and contains 40,017 protein-coding genes. Genomic analysis revealed that the <i>CYP716</i> family genes play a major role in platycoside oxidation. The <i>CYP716</i> gene family of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> was much larger than that of other Asterid species. Orthologous gene annotation also revealed the expansion of <i>β-amyrin synthases</i> (<i>bASs</i>) in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, which was confirmed by tissue-specific gene expression. In these expanded gene families, we identified key genes showing preferential expression in roots and association with platycoside biosynthesis. In addition, whole-genome bisulfite sequencing showed that <i>CYP716</i> and <i>bAS</i> genes are hypomethylated in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, suggesting that epigenetic modification of these two gene families affects platycoside biosynthesis. Thus whole-genome, transcriptome, and methylome data of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> provide novel insights into the regulation of platycoside biosynthesis by <i>CYP716</i> and <i>bAS</i> gene families.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Triterpenoid saponins (TSs) are naturally occurring, amphipathic, structurally, and functionally diverse phytochemicals consisting of triterpenoid or steroidal aglycones linked to oligosaccharide moieties<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Moses, T., Papadopoulou, K. K. &amp; Osbourn, A. Metabolic and functional diversity of saponins, biosynthetic intermediates and semi-synthetic derivatives. Crit. Rev. Biochem. Mol. Biol.49, 439–462 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d10656e800">1</a></sup>. TSs play important ecological functions, contribute to pest and pathogen resistance in plants, regulate crop quality, and are used for a wide range of applications in the pharmaceutical, pesticide, cosmetic, and food sectors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Moses, T., Papadopoulou, K. K. &amp; Osbourn, A. Metabolic and functional diversity of saponins, biosynthetic intermediates and semi-synthetic derivatives. Crit. Rev. Biochem. Mol. Biol.49, 439–462 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d10656e804">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Thimmappa, R., Geisler, K., Louveau, T., O’Maille, P. &amp; Osbourn, A. Triterpene biosynthesis in plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol.65, 225–257 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d10656e807">2</a></sup>. Previous studies indicate that TSs exhibit diverse number of therapeutic properties, including immunostimulatory, hypocholesterolemic, hepatoprotective, anticarcinogenic, neuroprotective, anti-inflammatory, antiviral, antiprotozoan, molluscicidal, and antioxidative properties<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Moses, T., Papadopoulou, K. K. &amp; Osbourn, A. Metabolic and functional diversity of saponins, biosynthetic intermediates and semi-synthetic derivatives. Crit. Rev. Biochem. Mol. Biol.49, 439–462 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d10656e811">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Glauert, A. M., Dingle, J. &amp; Lucy, J. Action of saponin on biological cell membranes. Nature196, 953–955 (1962)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d10656e814">3</a></sup>.</p><p>The structural diversity of TSs arises from their modular biosynthesis. First, TSs are synthesized via the mevalonic acid (MVA) and methylerythritol 4-phosphate (MEP) pathways by the cyclization of 2,3-oxidosqualene, giving rise to their oleanane- (β-amyrin) or dammarane-type triterpenoid skeletons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science345, 1181–1184 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10656e821">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhang, D. et al. The medicinal herb Panax notoginseng genome provides insights into ginsenoside biosynthesis and genome evolution. Mol. Plant10, 903–907 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10656e824">5</a></sup>. Then the triterpenoid skeletons undergo various modifications, such as oxidation, hydroxylation, or glycosylation, mediated by cytochrome P450 monooxygenases (CYP450s), UDP-glycosyltransferases (UGTs), and other enzymes. The CYP450 and UGT family proteins play essential roles in creating the structural diversity of TSs across plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Moses, T., Papadopoulou, K. K. &amp; Osbourn, A. Metabolic and functional diversity of saponins, biosynthetic intermediates and semi-synthetic derivatives. Crit. Rev. Biochem. Mol. Biol.49, 439–462 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d10656e828">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Thimmappa, R., Geisler, K., Louveau, T., O’Maille, P. &amp; Osbourn, A. Triterpene biosynthesis in plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol.65, 225–257 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d10656e831">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Seki, H., Tamura, K. &amp; Muranaka, T. P450s and UGTs: Key players in the structural diversity of triterpenoid saponins. Plant Cell Physiol.56, 1463–1471 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d10656e834">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Miettinen, K. et al. The ancient CYP716 family is a major contributor to the diversification of eudicot triterpenoid biosynthesis. Nat. Commun.8, 14153 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10656e837">7</a></sup>. For example, CYP716 family proteins synthesize oleanane-type TSs, platycosides, by catalyzing the C-28 oxidation of β-amyrin in <i>Platycodon grandiflorus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Miettinen, K. et al. The ancient CYP716 family is a major contributor to the diversification of eudicot triterpenoid biosynthesis. Nat. Commun.8, 14153 (2017)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10656e843">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tamura, K. et al. Cytochrome P450 monooxygenase CYP716A141 is a unique beta-amyrin C-16beta oxidase Involved in triterpenoid saponin biosynthesis in Platycodon grandiflorus. Plant Cell Physiol.58, 874–884 (2017)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10656e843_1">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Nyakudya, E., Jeong, J. H., Lee, N. K. &amp; Jeong, Y. S. Platycosides from the roots of Platycodon grandiflorum and their health benefits. Prev. Nutr. Food Sci.19, 59–68 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d10656e846">9</a></sup>.</p><p><i>P. grandiflorus</i> (2<i>n</i> = 18), a herbaceous perennial with bell-shaped flowers, belongs to the bellflower family, Campanulaceae. <i>P. grandiflorus</i> roots have been used as a popular food additive with therapeutic effects and also as a traditional medicine to treat respiratory diseases (bronchitis, asthma, tonsillitis, and pulmonary tuberculosis) and cold-related symptoms in East Asia for &gt;2000 years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, L. et al. Platycodon grandiflorus - an ethnopharmacological, phytochemical and pharmacological review. J. Ethnopharmacol.164, 147–161 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10656e861">10</a></sup>. Platycosides, especially platycodin D/E, are the most important bioactive compounds in the root extract of <i>P. grandiflorus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tamura, K. et al. Cytochrome P450 monooxygenase CYP716A141 is a unique beta-amyrin C-16beta oxidase Involved in triterpenoid saponin biosynthesis in Platycodon grandiflorus. Plant Cell Physiol.58, 874–884 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10656e867">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Ma, C. H. et al. Candidate genes involved in the biosynthesis of triterpenoid saponins in Platycodon grandiflorum identified by transcriptome analysis. Front. Plant Sci.7, 673 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d10656e870">11</a></sup>. Studies demonstrating the immunological and pharmacological properties of platycosides have generated considerable clinical interest in these compounds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Nyakudya, E., Jeong, J. H., Lee, N. K. &amp; Jeong, Y. S. Platycosides from the roots of Platycodon grandiflorum and their health benefits. Prev. Nutr. Food Sci.19, 59–68 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d10656e875">9</a></sup>. Platycosides exhibit anti-inflammatory, antiobesity, antiallergy, and antitumorigenic activities and inhibit hepatitis C infection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Nyakudya, E., Jeong, J. H., Lee, N. K. &amp; Jeong, Y. S. Platycosides from the roots of Platycodon grandiflorum and their health benefits. Prev. Nutr. Food Sci.19, 59–68 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d10656e879">9</a></sup>. Thus the level of platycosides in commercial products is the major factor that determines product quality<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Nyakudya, E., Jeong, J. H., Lee, N. K. &amp; Jeong, Y. S. Platycosides from the roots of Platycodon grandiflorum and their health benefits. Prev. Nutr. Food Sci.19, 59–68 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d10656e883">9</a></sup>. The platycosides of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> are structurally distinct from the dammarane-type TS of <i>Panax</i> species, ginsenoside<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xu, J. et al. Panax ginseng genome examination for ginsenoside biosynthesis. Gigascience6, 1–15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d10656e893">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Kim, N. H. et al. Genome and evolution of the shade-requiring medicinal herb Panax ginseng. Plant Biotechnol. J.16, 1904–1917 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d10656e896">13</a></sup>. This suggests that <i>P. grandiflorus</i> may be a useful medicinal plant model for studying evolution and molecular pathways related to the biosynthesis and production of oleanane-type TS by comparing with <i>Panax</i> species. For example, the preferential functions of β-amyrin synthase (<i>bAS</i>) in <i>P. grandiflorus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Miettinen, K. et al. The ancient CYP716 family is a major contributor to the diversification of eudicot triterpenoid biosynthesis. Nat. Commun.8, 14153 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10656e912">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tamura, K. et al. Cytochrome P450 monooxygenase CYP716A141 is a unique beta-amyrin C-16beta oxidase Involved in triterpenoid saponin biosynthesis in Platycodon grandiflorus. Plant Cell Physiol.58, 874–884 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10656e915">8</a></sup> and dammarenediol synthase (<i>DDS</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, L. et al. Platycodon grandiflorus - an ethnopharmacological, phytochemical and pharmacological review. J. Ethnopharmacol.164, 147–161 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10656e923">10</a></sup> in <i>Panax</i> species may imply that those genes are evolved specifically with the production of platycosides and ginsenosides, respectively. Moreover, CYP716 family is also known to evolve specifically toward the biosynthesis of TS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Miettinen, K. et al. The ancient CYP716 family is a major contributor to the diversification of eudicot triterpenoid biosynthesis. Nat. Commun.8, 14153 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10656e930">7</a></sup>. However, the evolutionary history of platycoside biosynthesis-related genes remains unknown, although ≥70 different platycosides have been isolated from <i>Platycodon</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Nyakudya, E., Jeong, J. H., Lee, N. K. &amp; Jeong, Y. S. Platycosides from the roots of Platycodon grandiflorum and their health benefits. Prev. Nutr. Food Sci.19, 59–68 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d10656e937">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, L. et al. Platycodon grandiflorus - an ethnopharmacological, phytochemical and pharmacological review. J. Ethnopharmacol.164, 147–161 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10656e940">10</a></sup>.</p><p>Here we report the whole-genome assembly, transcriptome, and methylome of <i>P. grandiflorus</i>. This genome-wide analysis revealed that the <i>CYP716</i> and <i>bAS</i> gene families play a major role in platycoside biosynthesis with species-specific expansion. Furthermore, the genomic data were supported by the methylome data, indicating the role of epigenetics in platycoside biosynthesis. Overall, our results provide key insights into the evolutionary expansion and transcriptional regulation of platycoside biosynthesis genes in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly of <i>P. grandiflorus</i>
</h3><p><i>P. grandiflorus</i> cultivar Jangbaek-doraji was used for whole-genome sequencing (WGS) after four generations of self-fertilization. Karyotype analysis confirmed that <i>P. grandiflorus</i> has a diploid genome (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 18) with 4 metacentric chromosome pairs (length: 2.19–2.57 μm) and 5 sub-metacentric chromosome pairs (length: 1.91–3.48 μm) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S1</a>). In addition, <i>k</i>-mer analysis estimated the genome size of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> as approximately 683.3 Mb (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S2</a>). A hybrid assembly of short and long reads resulted in a 680.1 Mb draft genome, with 4815 scaffolds (N50 value = 277.1 kb) and 1.35% gaps (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S3</a>). The draft genome assembly captured 96.9% of the complete Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCOs) with the Virdiplantae_odb10 database (update date: 20-11-2019) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S4</a>); 84.9%, 12.0%, 2.4%, and 0.7% of BUSCO genes were predicted as complete and single-copy, complete and duplicated, fragmented, and missing, respectively (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S17</a>). However, erroneous assembly by duplication was assessed to be 12% even if showing high level of complete BUSCOs (scores for duplicated BUSCOs are provided in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S18</a>). We also validated the assembly by comparing the read spectrum with the copy number in the assembly using KAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Mapleson, D., Garcia Accinelli, G., Kettleborough, G., Wright, J. &amp; Clavijo, B. KAT: a K-mer analysis toolkit to quality control NGS datasets and genome assemblies. Bioinformatics33, 574–576 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1018">14</a></sup>. The <i>k</i>-mer showed the homozygous distribution without a certain heterozygous peak (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S16</a>), consistent with the low heterozygosity observed by GenomeScope (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S2c</a>). Furthermore, the absent <i>k</i>-mers (black in <i>k</i>-mer comparison plot) at the frequency of average sampling depth was relatively low (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S16</a>), suggesting a good quality of assembly completeness. In addition, we aligned short reads to itself. The results showed that 98.1% of the short reads (816,532,377 mapped reads out of 831,574,902 clean raw reads) were successfully re-aligned to the assembly, with 98% effective coverage (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S4a, b</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S20</a>), and the unassembled genomic fraction was only 2%. The analysis also showed 0.44% of the nucleotides in the <i>P. grandiflorus</i> genome were heterozygous (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S4c</a>), indicating the low heterozygous characteristics of the draft genome assembly of <i>P. grandiflorus</i>. Overall, the genome assembly of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> was of good quality.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Genome assembly and gene prediction of <i>Platycodon grandiflorus</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation of <i>P. grandiflorus</i>
</h3><p>A total of 40,017 non-redundant (NR) protein-coding genes were predicted in the <i>P. grandiflorus</i> genome, with an average length of 5051 bp from repeat-masked genomic sequence (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S3</a>) using evidence-driven gene prediction methods coupled with ab initio prediction. The BUSCO analysis for the gene set showed that 83.3%, 7.1%, 7.8%, and 1.9% of BUSCO genes were predicted as complete and single-copy, complete and duplicated, fragmented, and missing, respectively (the part of gene set in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S4</a>; BUSCO scores are listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S19</a>). The gene models were supported by 89.1% PacBio isoform sequencing (Iso-Seq) data, comprising 92,368 assembled isoforms derived from leaf, stem, and root tissues (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S6a, b</a>) and 98.1% Illumina RNA-Seq data of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> derived from 8 different tissues (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S7</a>); these data indicate that genes were predicted with a high level of confidence. Gene annotation revealed a high abundance of genes associated with lipid metabolism and carbohydrate biosynthesis (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S9</a>). This trend was conserved among the genomes of five herbal species, including sunflower (<i>Helianthus annuus</i>), coffee (<i>Coffea canephora</i>), carrot (<i>Daucus carota</i>), and <i>Panax ginseng</i>. We also identified a total of 9027 genes encoding transcription factors (TFs) in the <i>P. grandiflorus</i> genome, with a predominance of genes encoding basic helix-loop-helix (bHLH) TFs (11%) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S10</a>). Interestingly, four <i>triterpene saponin biosynthesis activation regulator</i> (<i>TSAR</i>) duplicates in the bHLH family were identified (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S5</a>); the <i>TSAR</i> genes regulate β-subunit of tryptophan synthase in alfalfa (<i>Medicago truncatula</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Mertens, J. et al. The bHLH transcription factors TSAR1 and TSAR2 regulate triterpene saponin biosynthesis in Medicago truncatula. Plant Physiol.170, 194–210 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1247">15</a></sup> and quinoa (<i>Chenopodium quinoa</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jarvis, D. E. et al. The genome of Chenopodium quinoa. Nature542, 307–312 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1255">16</a></sup>. Among the four gene duplicates, only PGJG172350 (<i>TSAR2</i> homolog) was expressed in root, stem, flower, and leaf tissues of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S5</a>), suggesting its role in the regulation of platycoside biosynthesis. In addition, evidence-based de novo prediction indicated that 36.2% (248 Mb) of the <i>P. grandiflorus</i> genome was composed of transposable elements (TEs) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S5</a>). This value was lower than that in other Asterid species, including carrot (46%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat. Genet.48, 657–666 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1275">17</a></sup>, coffee (50%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science345, 1181–1184 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1279">4</a></sup>, and <i>P. ginseng</i> (80%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xu, J. et al. Panax ginseng genome examination for ginsenoside biosynthesis. Gigascience6, 1–15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1286">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Kim, N. H. et al. Genome and evolution of the shade-requiring medicinal herb Panax ginseng. Plant Biotechnol. J.16, 1904–1917 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1289">13</a></sup>, suggesting that the genome structure of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> is relatively simple.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Evolution of the <i>P. grandiflorus</i> genome in the Asterid lineage</h3><p>To understand the evolutionary history of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> in the Asterid II lineage, we conducted a genome-wide orthologous gene comparison among Asterid II species, including <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, <i>P. ginseng</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xu, J. et al. Panax ginseng genome examination for ginsenoside biosynthesis. Gigascience6, 1–15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1316">12</a></sup>, <i>Panax notoginseng</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhang, D. et al. The medicinal herb Panax notoginseng genome provides insights into ginsenoside biosynthesis and genome evolution. Mol. Plant10, 903–907 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1322">5</a></sup>, carrot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat. Genet.48, 657–666 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1327">17</a></sup>, and sunflower<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature546, 148–152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1331">18</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S6</a>), while the coffee<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science345, 1181–1184 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1338">4</a></sup> genome was used as an Asterid I representative. Of the 25,929 orthologous gene families identified in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, 5498 gene families were conserved among Asterid II species (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S11</a>). We then constructed a phylogenetic tree based on 606 single-copy orthologs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig1">1a</a>). This tree assigned <i>P. grandiflorus</i> in the early Asterid II lineage, consistent with a previous study showing early divergence in the Campanulaceae family based on chloroplast genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hong, C. P. et al. accD nuclear transfer of Platycodon grandiflorum and the plastid of early Campanulaceae. BMC Genomics18, 607 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1355">19</a></sup>. Based on these findings, we analyzed the expansion and contraction of gene families according to their evolutionary histories. In <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, 13 gene families showed expansion relative to the most recent common ancestor (MRCA) of Asterids, which arose 104 million years ago (MYA), and 63 gene families showed expansion relative to the MRCA of Asterids II, which diverged 92 MYA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig1">1a</a>). Of the 63 gene families that were expanded relative to the MRCA of Asterids II, 9 gene families contained various functional domains, including zinc knuckle, legume lectin domain, transferase, salt stress response/antifungal, F-box domain, protein kinase domain, CYP450, protein tyrosine kinase, and UDP-glucoronosyl/UDP-glucosyl transferase (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S13</a>). Interestingly, our in-depth annotation revealed expansion of CYP71A/B, CYP72, CYP76C, and CYP716 families shared within the Asterid clade (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S7</a>). For example, CYP71A/B families expanded in the MRCA of Asterids; CYP72, CYP76C, and CYP716 families expanded in the MRCA of Asterids II (right panel in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig1">1a</a>); and CYP82 and CYP75 families contracted in the MRCA of Asterids II (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S8</a>). Furthermore, the analysis revealed species-specific expansion and contraction of those families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig1">1a</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S8</a>), suggesting their recursive expansions and contractions and its genetic divergence. Remarkably, CYP76C, CYP72, and CYP716 families were specifically expanded in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>. In the CYP716 family, CYP716A and CYP716S subfamilies were abundant in <i>P. grandiflorus</i> relative to other Asterid species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig1">1b</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S9</a>). Thus our results provide novel insights into the evolution of the <i>P. grandiflorus</i> genome and CYP450 subfamilies.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Evolution of the CYP716 family in P. grandiflorus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Evolution of the CYP716 family in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0329-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_329_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0329-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_329_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1232" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Expansion and contraction of the CYP716 family in <i>P. grandiflorus</i> based on a time-calibrated phylogeny of six Asterid species. Branch numbers indicate the number of expanded (blue) and contracted (red) gene families after the split from the most recent common ancestor (MRCA). The table (right) shows the number of CYP450 families identified in Asterids analyzed on the left. In the table, the gray shaded regions indicate expansions of the CYP450 family in the MRCA, and red-color numbers indicate expansions specific to the corresponding species. <b>b</b> Heatmap showing the abundance of CYP716 proteins in six Asterids. Asterisks (*) indicate CYP716 proteins that modify triterpene scaffolds. <b>c</b> β-Amyrin modifications in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>. The structure of PubChem (ID: 73145) was modified to use the β-amyrin scaffold. Different reactions involved in the modification of β-amyrin are indicated in red, blue, and green in <b>d</b>. <b>d</b> Heatmap showing the expression of <i>CYP450</i> genes and known reactions on platycoside scaffolds. Gene expression is presented as log<sub>2</sub> fragments per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads (FPKM) of RNA-Seq data from eight different tissues. Asterisks (*) indicate genes with significantly higher expression in roots than in other tissues (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.001; Fisher’s exact test)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Expansion of the <i>CYP716</i> family contributes to the diversification of platycoside scaffolds in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>
</h3><p>We identified a total of 158 putative TS scaffold-modifying genes, belonging to CYP716, CYP72, CYP87, and CYP93 families, in 7 plant species, including <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, <i>P. ginseng</i>, <i>P. notoginseng</i>, carrot, sunflower, coffee, and grape (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S9</a>). This analysis revealed different distribution patterns of those families in six Asterid species and in grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>), thus possibly contributing to TS diversification, consistent with previous results<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ghosh, S. Triterpene structural diversification by plant cytochrome P450 enzymes. Front. Plant Sci.8, 1886 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1476">20</a></sup>. Of the genes identified in <i>P. grandiflorus</i> (35 genes out of 158), the <i>CYP716A</i> subfamily genes were the most abundant, comprising 19 paralogs within 7 subfamilies, all of which were specialized in the C-28 oxidation of the β-amyrin skeleton (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig1">1c</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S9</a>). The CYP716A subfamily seems to play a major role in the diversification of scaffolds involved in platycoside biosynthesis in <i>P. grandiflorus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Miettinen, K. et al. The ancient CYP716 family is a major contributor to the diversification of eudicot triterpenoid biosynthesis. Nat. Commun.8, 14153 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1495">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Nyakudya, E., Jeong, J. H., Lee, N. K. &amp; Jeong, Y. S. Platycosides from the roots of Platycodon grandiflorum and their health benefits. Prev. Nutr. Food Sci.19, 59–68 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1498">9</a></sup>. Three <i>CYP716A</i> genes, including <i>CYP716A12</i>, <i>CYP716A140</i>, and <i>CYP716A75</i>, were identified only in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>. In addition to the <i>CYP716A</i> subfamily, the <i>CYP716S5</i> subfamily was also specifically expanded in <i>P. grandiflorus</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S9</a>). The CYP716S5 proteins are involved in the epoxidation at C12-C13α of β-amyrin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Miettinen, K. et al. The ancient CYP716 family is a major contributor to the diversification of eudicot triterpenoid biosynthesis. Nat. Commun.8, 14153 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1530">7</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig1">1c</a>, blue) and play a role in producing the heterocyclic saponin, 12α-hydroxy-β-amyrin-13,28β-lactone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Miettinen, K. et al. The ancient CYP716 family is a major contributor to the diversification of eudicot triterpenoid biosynthesis. Nat. Commun.8, 14153 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1538">7</a></sup>. The cytotoxic effects of the heterocyclic saponin have been demonstrated in the human ECA-109 cell line. However, only a small amount of the heterocyclic saponin was isolated from the roots of <i>P. grandiflorus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhang, L., Liu, Z.-H. &amp; Tian, J.-K. Cytotoxic triterpenoid saponins from the roots of Platycodon grandiflorum. Molecules12, 832–841 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1544">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Kwon, J. et al. Effect of processing method on platycodin D content in Platycodon grandiflorum roots. Arch. Pharm. Res.40, 1087–1093 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1547">22</a></sup>; thus a better extraction method is needed to isolate a sufficient amount of this chemical for clinical use. The <i>CYP72A154</i> subfamily was also specifically expanded in <i>P. grandiflorus</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig1">1c</a>, green<b>)</b><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Seki, H. et al. Triterpene functional genomics in licorice for identification of CYP72A154 involved in the biosynthesis of glycyrrhizin. Plant Cell23, 4112–4123 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1563">23</a></sup>; CYP72A154 proteins catalyze the C-30 hydroxylation of the β-amyrin skeleton, although their role in platycoside biosynthesis is not as important as that of CYP716 proteins (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S9</a>). Therefore, our results suggest that the <i>CYP716</i> gene family evolved specifically in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, thus contributing to the diversification of platycoside scaffolds, given its oxidation activity.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Divergent expression of the <i>CYP716</i> family genes in different tissues of <i>P. grandiflorus</i>
</h3><p>Expression analysis of 35 <i>CYP716</i> family genes in different <i>P. grandiflorus</i> tissues, including leaf, root, stem, seed, petal, pistil, sepal, and stamen (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S2</a>), revealed tissue-specific expression profiles (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S10</a>). Four <i>CYP716</i> family genes including PGJG182820, PGJG233930, PGJG086700, and PGJG310130 showed significantly higher expression in roots than in other tissues (<i>p</i> values: 3.8 × 10<sup>−8</sup>, 5.9 × 10<sup>−6</sup>, 5.0 × 10<sup>−14</sup>, 1.3 × 10<sup>−12</sup>, respectively), whereas other <i>CYP716</i> family genes were specifically expressed in other tissues; for example, PGJG266060 in seeds (<i>p</i> = 1.2 × 10<sup>−49</sup>), PGJG054290 in petals (<i>p</i> = 5.2 × 10<sup>−29</sup>), and PGJG016220 in stamen (<i>p</i> = 2.9 × 10<sup>−4</sup>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig1">1d</a>). Besides <i>CYP716</i> genes, we identified four <i>CYP716S5</i> genes, of which PGJG153470 exhibited higher expression in flower tissues, including pistil (<i>p</i> = 4.9 × 10<sup>−4</sup>), sepal (<i>p</i> = 1.4 × 10<sup>−5</sup>), and stamen (<i>p</i> = 3.0 × 10<sup>−16</sup>), than in roots (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig1">1d</a>). Therefore, the divergent tissue-specific expression patterns of <i>CYP716</i> family genes, along with <i>bAS</i> genes, lead to the accumulation of platycosides to varying levels in different tissues of <i>P. grandiflorus</i>. In addition, our results suggest that <i>CYP716</i> paralogs may be sub-functionalized in various tissues for platycoside biosynthesis.</p><p>Exogenous application of methyl jasmonate (MeJA or MJ) increases the expression of triterpenoid saponin biosynthesis (TSB)-related genes in <i>Panax</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wang, J., Gao, W., Zuo, B., Zhang, L. &amp; Huang, L. Effect of methyl jasmonate on the ginsenoside content of Panax ginseng adventitious root cultures and on the genes involved in triterpene biosynthesis. Res. Chem. Intermed.39, 1973–1980 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1686">24</a></sup>. Therefore, we examined the expression of <i>CYP716</i> family genes in <i>P. grandiflorus</i> plants treated with MJ for 12, 24, and 48 h. Two <i>CYP716</i> family genes including PGJG086700 (<i>CYP716A140</i>) and PGJG310130 (<i>CYP716A141</i>) were highly expressed in roots at all three time points in response to the MJ treatment (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S10</a>), indicating their association with the induction of platycoside biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, W., Zhao, Z. J., Xu, Y., Qian, X. &amp; Zhong, J. J. Efficient induction of ginsenoside biosynthesis and alteration of ginsenoside heterogeneity in cell cultures of Panax notoginseng by using chemically synthesized 2-hydroxyethyl jasmonate. Appl. Microbiol. Biotechnol.70, 298–307 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1709">25</a></sup>. Moreover, both these genes are involved in oleanolic acid biosynthesis, as shown previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Miettinen, K. et al. The ancient CYP716 family is a major contributor to the diversification of eudicot triterpenoid biosynthesis. Nat. Commun.8, 14153 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1713">7</a></sup>. Therefore, our transcriptome data can be useful for researchers to identify and confirm key genes involved in TSB.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genomic expansion and divergent expression of TSB-related genes in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>
</h3><p>We manually selected genes involved in various TSB pathways, including MVA, MEP, isopentenyl pyrophosphate, and OSC pathways (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S11</a>), and analyzed conserved domains in the proteins encoded by these genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S12</a>). Based on protein domain and phylogenetic analyses, a total of 827 TSB-related genes were identified in 7 model plant species analyzed in the study (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S15</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S13</a>). Furthermore, we analyzed the expression of TSB-related genes in eight tissues of plants treated with MJ for three different time periods (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S14</a>). A similar gene number was identified in the MVA and MEP pathways of six Asterid species and grape (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S13</a>). Interestingly, expansion of the <i>geranylgeranyl pyrophosphate synthase</i> (<i>GGPS</i>) gene family was identified in <i>P. grandiflorus</i> (32 genes, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig2">2a</a>); these genes function downstream of the MVA and MEP pathways (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig2">2c</a>). The <i>GGPS</i> gene family of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> was twofold larger than that of <i>P. ginseng</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhang, D. et al. The medicinal herb Panax notoginseng genome provides insights into ginsenoside biosynthesis and genome evolution. Mol. Plant10, 903–907 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1772">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xu, J. et al. Panax ginseng genome examination for ginsenoside biosynthesis. Gigascience6, 1–15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1775">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Kim, N. H. et al. Genome and evolution of the shade-requiring medicinal herb Panax ginseng. Plant Biotechnol. J.16, 1904–1917 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1778">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chen, W. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and analysis of the Chinese herbal plant Panax notoginseng. Mol. Plant10, 899–902 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1781">26</a></sup>. Phylogenetic analysis revealed a <i>GGPS</i> gene cluster in <i>P. grandiflorus</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig2">2a</a>), suggesting that the expansion of the <i>GGPS</i> gene family in <i>P. grandiflorus</i> was relatively recent. Despite the considerable amount of gene duplication in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, many <i>GGPS</i> paralogs (20/32) were not expressed in any tissue, being further validated by quantitative reverse transcriptase polymerase chain reaction (qRT-PCR) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S15</a>) and supported by high correlation between RNA-Seq and qRT-PCR (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.952–1.0 in root, leaf, stem, and flower). Five <i>GGPS</i> genes showed significantly higher expression in flowers and leaves than in other tissues (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig2">2c</a>; Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S15</a>): PGJG000490 (leaf, <i>p</i> = 5.27 × 10<sup>−8</sup>; stamen, <i>p</i> = 4.75 × 10<sup>−10</sup>), PGJG339290 (stamen, <i>p</i> = 3.80 × 10<sup>−53</sup>), PGJG305170 (seed, <i>p</i> = 3.36 × 10<sup>−17</sup>), PGJG304840 (sepal, <i>p</i> = 0.0016), and PGJG167180 (stamen, <i>p</i> = 1.42 × 10<sup>−12</sup>). We also identified a relatively high number of <i>bAS</i> duplicates (24 copies) in <i>P. grandiflorus</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig2">2b, c</a>); these genes synthesize the precursor of oleanane-type TS (β-amyrin) by cyclizing 2,3-oxidosqualene. The <i>bAS</i> gene duplicates also showed tissue-specific expression profiles. Of the 24 <i>bAS</i> genes, 4 genes, including PGJG046170, PGJG204380, PGJG290560, and PGJG099090, showed significantly higher expression in roots than in other tissues (<i>p</i> values: 3.67 × 10<sup>−4</sup>, 1.11 × 10<sup>−53</sup>, 1.89 × 10<sup>−5</sup>, and 5.5 × 10<sup>−3</sup>, respectively; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig2">2c</a>). In addition, we identified four <i>dammarenediol synthase</i> (<i>DDS</i>) genes in the <i>P. grandiflorus</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig2">2b, c</a>); the <i>DDS</i> genes synthesize the scaffold of the dammarane-type TS enriched in <i>Panax</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Kim, N. H. et al. Genome and evolution of the shade-requiring medicinal herb Panax ginseng. Plant Biotechnol. J.16, 1904–1917 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d10656e1907">13</a></sup>. However, all of these <i>DDS</i> genes showed low level of expression in roots, which may be associated with less attention paid to the dammarane-type TS in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>. Altogether, our results demonstrate the expansion of <i>GGPS</i> and <i>bAS</i> gene families in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, and their divergent expression patterns in various tissues, similar to the <i>CYP716</i> family genes. In addition, the identification of TSB-related candidate genes with tissue-specific expression profiles may help elucidate their targets and molecular mechanism underlying platycoside biosynthesis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig2">2c</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Expansion and diverged expression patterns of triterpenoid saponin biosynthesis (TBS)-related genes in P. grandiflorus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Expansion and diverged expression patterns of triterpenoid saponin biosynthesis (TBS)-related genes in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0329-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_329_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0329-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_329_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="750" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <b>b</b> Phylogeny of the <i>GGPS</i> (<b>a</b>) and <i>bAS</i> (<b>b</b>) gene families in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>. Phylogenetic trees were constructed using the maximum likelihood (ML) method. <b>c</b> Expression profiles of TBS-related genes in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>. Data represent log<sub>2</sub> FPKM values of RNA-Seq data generated from eight different tissues, including root, leaf, stem, seed, and four flowers, of plants treated with methyl jasmonate (MJ) for 12, 24, and 48 h (MJ 12 h, MJ 24 h, and MJ 48 h, respectively); CT 12 h indicates the control</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Hypomethylation of <i>CYP716</i> and <i>bAS</i> genes of <i>P. grandiflorus</i>
</h3><p>To understand the link between DNA methylation and TSB regulation in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, we performed whole-genome bisulfite sequencing (WGBS) and analyzed the DNA methylation status of TSB-related genes. We generated 7.5–10.7 Gb of whole-genome methylome data from MJ-treated <i>P. grandiflorus</i> samples harvested at 3 time points (12, 24, and 48 h), covering &gt;90% of all cytosine nucleotides (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S16</a>). Average global methylation levels were estimated at 89.1%, 72.4%, and 18.9% in the CG, CHG, and CHH contexts, respectively, of a control (CT 12 h) and 3 MJ treatments (MJ 12 h, MJ 24 h, and MJ 48 h) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S16</a>). The average methylation levels of <i>CYP450</i> family and other TSB-related genes differed from those of the remaining protein-coding genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3a</a>); the CG methylation level of <i>CYP450</i> family genes was lower in gene bodies but higher within ~2-kb upstream and downstream regions, whereas the CHG and CHH methylation levels were slightly increased in gene bodies. However, we could not find any global changes in methylation levels between control and MJ treatments (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3a</a>), indicating only a small number of differentially methylated cytosines (DMCs). The <i>CYP450</i> and other TSB-related genes showed higher density of CG methylation than that of CHG/CHH methylation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3b</a>), indicating the epigenetic role of CG methylation in TS biosynthesis. Of the 9845 CG-DMCs identified between control and MJ treatments, 5140, 5512, and 5461 CpG sites showed differential methylation levels in the MJ treatment at 12, 24, and 48 h, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3c</a>, top panel). The CG-DMCs were the most predominant among the <i>CYP76</i> (8.9%), <i>CYP716</i> (8.7%), <i>CYP72</i> (7.4%), <i>CYP71</i> (6.9%), <i>GGPS</i> (8.0%), and <i>bAS</i> (7.7%) genes, which were abundant within the top 30% <i>CYP450</i> families and other TSB-related genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3c</a>, bottom panel). In addition, 16% of all CG-DMCs were detected at all three time points of the MJ treatment (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3c</a>), indicating continuous changes in CG methylation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="DNA methylation landscape of CYP450 family and other TSB-related genes in P. grandiflorus genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: DNA methylation landscape of <i>CYP450</i> family and other TSB-related genes in <i>P. grandiflorus</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0329-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_329_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0329-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_329_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="762" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Methylation status of the <i>CYP450</i> family genes in the CG, CHG, and CHH contexts. Based on the distribution of methylation levels of all protein-coding genes (including gene bodies and ~2-kb upstream and downstream regions) in the control sample (CT 12 h_AllGenes; black line), the methylation levels of <i>CYP450</i> genes were measured in the control treatment (CT 12 h; red) and at three time points in the MJ treatment (MJ 12 h [blue], MJ 24 h [green], and MJ 48 h [orange]). <b>b</b> Density of differentially methylated cytosines (DMCs) in all three contexts in <i>CYP450</i> and other TSB-related genes between the control and MJ treatments (CT vs. MJ 12 h, CT vs. MJ 24 h, and CT vs. MJ 48 h). <b>c</b> Abundance of CG-DMCs in the top 30% <i>CYP450</i> and TSB-related genes in the MJ treatment. <b>d</b> Relative abundance of hypermethylated and hypomethylated cytosines (hyper-DMCs and hypo-DMCs, respectively) in the CG context of <i>CYP716s</i>, <i>CYP72s</i>, <i>CYP76s</i>, <i>CYP71s</i>, <i>GGPSs</i>, and <i>bAS</i> in the MJ treatments. The ratio was transformed to the log<sub>2</sub> scale. <b>e</b> Relative abundance of hyper-DMCs and hypo-DMCs in the CG context within ~2-kb upstream regions and coding regions of the primary <i>CYP450</i> and TSB-related genes. <b>f</b> Identification of genes showing inverse correlation between CG methylation levels and gene expression in the MJ treatment. Asterisk (*) indicates a gene with similar DMC distribution in the ~2-kb upstream region and coding region</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We further examined the relative abundance of hypomethylated cytosines (hypo-DMCs) and hypermethylated cytosines (hyper-DMCs) in the <i>CYP450</i> family and other TSB-related genes. Among the six gene families ranked within the top 30%, the <i>CYP716</i> and <i>bAS</i> gene families showed relatively lower CG methylation in the MJ treatment (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3d</a>). Furthermore, the relative dominance of hypo-CG-DMCs in the MJ treatment was detected at all three time points in the upstream and coding regions of <i>CYP716</i> family genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3e</a>) but only at 48 h in the upstream and coding regions of <i>bAS</i> genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3e</a>). We also identified 22 genes, belonging to <i>CYP716</i>, <i>CYP72</i>, <i>CYP71</i>, <i>bAS</i>, and <i>GGPS</i> families, showing inverse correlation between CG methylation and gene expression in the MJ treatment (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3f</a>). This inverse correlation was detected in the upstream and coding regions of two genes, including PGJG182820 (<i>CYP716A</i>) and PGJG255920 (<i>bAS</i>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3f</a>, asterisk), suggesting a possible connection between epigenetic modification and transcriptional changes. In addition, we observed the relative dominance of hyper-CG-DMCs in the upstream regions of <i>GGPSs</i>, resulting in low or no expression of these genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#Fig3">3f</a>). Overall, our results suggest that <i>CYP716</i> and <i>bAS</i> family genes are likely hypomethylated in <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, indicating that epigenetic changes in both these gene families affect platycoside biosynthesis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p><i>P. grandiflorus</i> is used as a popular medicinal and dietary resource throughout East Asia, given its therapeutic effects. Compared with the previously published genomes of six Asterid species, the draft genome assembly of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> revealed an expansion of <i>CYP716</i> and <i>bAS</i> gene families during evolution, which may explain the biosynthesis of platycosides and their divergent chemical structures. The transcriptome data were useful for identifying essential TSB-related candidate genes associated with the production of platycosides in various tissues, especially roots. Furthermore, WGBS provided evidence suggesting epigenetic modification of <i>CYP716</i> and <i>bAS</i> genes. Thus the <i>P. grandiflorus</i> genome will serve as a valuable resource for studying genes needed to improve agricultural traits and platycoside production efficiency through molecular breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant material and WGS</h3><p><i>P. grandiflorus</i> cultivar Jangbaek-doraji was used in this study after four generations of self-fertilization. Plants of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> were grown for 1 year in a bellflower field in the Department of Herbal Crop Research, Rural Development Administration (RDA), Republic of Korea. Karyotyping of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> was performed by fluorescence in situ hybridization analysis using two probes, 5S rDNA and 45S rDNA. Genomic DNA was isolated from young leaves of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> plants using the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen, USA). WGS of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> was performed using the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform and TruSeq Synthetic Long Read (TSLR) technology to generate short reads (insert sizes: 270, 500, 700, and 360 bp) and long reads (average read length: 6.8 kb), respectively, according to the manufacturer’s instructions (Illumina, Inc., San Diego, CA) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S1</a>). The Illumina HisSeq 2500 platform was also used to generate long mate pair reads (insert sizes: 2, 5, and 10 kb) for generating scaffolds (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S1</a>). The WGS of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> generated 325.5 Gb of Illumina short reads (476.4× coverage) and 4 Gb of TruSeq synthetic long reads (TSLRs; 5.9× coverage) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Genome assembly</h3><p>The genome size of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> was estimated by the <i>k</i>-mer frequency analysis using SOAPec<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Luo, R. et al. Erratum: SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience4, 30 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2282">28</a></sup> (version 2.01), Jellyfish (version 2.2.0), and GenomeScope (<a href="http://qb.cshl.edu/genomescope">http://qb.cshl.edu/genomescope</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ranallo-Benavidez, T. R., Jaron, K. S. &amp; Schatz, M. C. GenomeScope 2.0 and Smudgeplot for reference-free profiling of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun.11, 1–10 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2290">29</a></sup>. For <i>k</i>-mer analysis, sequencing errors in short-read libraries (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S1</a>) were corrected using SOAPec (version 2.01). After error correction, a peak (31 depth) in the <i>k</i>-mer depth distribution was identified.
