The whole genome of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> was assembled de novo using a hybrid approach involving short and long reads (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S3</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S3</a>). A total of 132 Gb of short reads were error-corrected using SOAPec<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Luo, R. et al. Erratum: SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience4, 30 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2314">28</a></sup> and assembled into contigs using SOAPdenovo2 (version 2.04)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Luo, R. et al. Erratum: SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience4, 30 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2318">28</a></sup>, generating 1,450,347 contigs (528 Mb) with an N50 value of 3.5 kb. Long mate pair reads (193 Gb) were used to join contigs into scaffolds using SOAPdenovo2, and gaps within scaffolds were filled using GapFiller<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Nadalin, F., Vezzi, F. &amp; Policriti, A. GapFiller: a de novo assembly approach to fill the gap within paired reads. BMC Bioinformatics13, S8 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2322">30</a></sup>, generating a total of 18,379 scaffolds (633 Mb; N50 = 363 kb). TSLRs (3.99 Gb) were separately assembled into contigs using Celera Assembler (version 8.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Denisov, G. et al. Consensus generation and variant detection by Celera Assembler. Bioinformatics24, 1035–1040 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2326">31</a></sup>, generating 68,092 contigs (633 Mb; N50 = 15 kb). Assemblies generated from short and long reads were merged using GARM (version 0.7.5) and CAP3. The resulting contigs were further scaffolded with short reads, long reads, and long mate pair reads using SSPACE (version 3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2330">32</a></sup> and SSPACE-LongRead (version 1.1), followed by gap filling using GapFiller (version 1.10)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Nadalin, F., Vezzi, F. &amp; Policriti, A. GapFiller: a de novo assembly approach to fill the gap within paired reads. BMC Bioinformatics13, S8 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2334">30</a></sup>. The completeness of the genome assembly was assessed using BUSCO (version 4.0.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2339">33</a></sup> with 425 BUSCOs of viridiplantae_odb10 database. The workflow of de novo assembly is summarized in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM1">S3</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">RNA-Seq and data analysis</h3><p>Total RNA was isolated from eight tissues of <i>P. grandiflorus</i>, including leaves, stems, roots, petals, sepals, pistils, stamens, and seeds, and seedlings of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> were treated with MJ for 12, 24, and 48 h, with a control, using a total RNA extraction kit (Intron Biotechnology, Korea). RNA-Seq libraries were prepared from those RNA samples using the TruSeq Stranded mRNA Library Prep Kit (Illumina, Inc., San Diego, CA, USA) according to the manufacturer’s protocol. Paired-end sequencing with 100 cycles was performed using an Illumina HiSeq 2500 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S2</a>). Clean reads were mapped onto the draft genome of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> using TopHat. Gene expression levels were estimated in terms of FPKM (fragments per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads) values using Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc.7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2369">27</a></sup> based on gene annotations, and tissue-specific expression levels were compared using Fisher’s exact test. Genes differentially expressed between the control (CT 12 h) and MJ treatments (MJ 12 h/24 h/48 h) (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.01; |fold-change| ≥ 1.5) were analyzed using Cuffdiff<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc.7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2377">27</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Gene prediction and gene annotation</h3><p>A combination of ab initio and evidence-based approaches was used for gene prediction. The genome assembly was pre-masked for repetitive DNA sequences using RepeatMasker (version 4.0.6) (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>). An unsupervised training gene structure was generated using GeneMark-ET (version 4.10)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lomsadze, A., Burns, P. D. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Integration of mapped RNA-Seq reads into automatic training of eukaryotic gene finding algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res.42, e119 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2393">34</a></sup> by incorporating RNA-Seq and Iso-Seq data. Gene prediction was further performed using AUGUSTUS (version 3.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics24, 637–644 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2397">35</a></sup>, based on gene structure information, such as exon–intron boundaries, predicted from RNA-Seq and homologous protein sequence alignment data. Gene models were predicted based on RNA-Seq and Iso-Seq data using TopHat (version 2.1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics25, 1105–1111 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2401">36</a></sup>, and amino acid sequence alignments with the NCBI NR protein database were performed using Exonerate (version 2.4.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Slater, G. S. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2405">37</a></sup>. To validate the accuracy of gene models, RNA-Seq and Iso-Seq data were aligned with gene models using TopHat and GMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics21, 1859–1875 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2410">38</a></sup>. To perform functional gene annotation, gene models were searched against UniProt, NCBI NR, and Plant RefSeq databases using BLASTP (version 2.3.0+) (<i>E</i>-value cutoff = 1<i>E</i> − 5). Protein domains were searched using InterProScan (version 5.19–58.0). Functional annotation of gene models was performed using gene ontology (<a href="http://www.geneontology.org">www.geneontology.org</a>) enrichment analysis, Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (<a href="https://www.genome.jp/kegg">https://www.genome.jp/kegg</a>) database, Plant Metabolic Pathway database (<a href="https://www.plantcyc.org">https://www.plantcyc.org</a>), and Plant Transcription Factor Database (PlantTFDB; <a href="http://planttfdb.cbi.pku.edu.cn/">http://planttfdb.cbi.pku.edu.cn/</a>). TE sequences were identified using RepeatMasker. To identify TE-related coding sequences, non-repeat masked assembled sequences were searched against the MIPS Repeat Element Database (mipsREdat_9.3p; <a href="http://www.transplantdb.eu/node/2249">http://www.transplantdb.eu/node/2249</a>) using TBLASTX and BLASTN (version 2.3.0+) (<i>E</i>-value cutoff = 1<i>E</i> − 20).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Gene family expansions and contractions</h3><p>Genomic resources of one Asterid I species (<i>Coffea canephora</i> [Cca]<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science345, 1181–1184 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2459">4</a></sup>); four Asterid II species, including <i>P. ginseng</i> (Pgi)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xu, J. et al. Panax ginseng genome examination for ginsenoside biosynthesis. Gigascience6, 1–15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2466">12</a></sup>, <i>P. notoginseng</i> (Pno)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhang, D. et al. The medicinal herb Panax notoginseng genome provides insights into ginsenoside biosynthesis and genome evolution. Mol. Plant10, 903–907 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2474">5</a></sup>, <i>D. carota</i> (Dca)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat. Genet.48, 657–666 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2481">17</a></sup>, and <i>H. annuus</i> (Han)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature546, 148–152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2488">18</a></sup>; and one outgroup species (<i>V. vinifera</i> [Vvi])<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2496">39</a></sup> were used to investigate the genomic and evolutionary features of <i>P</i>. <i>grandiflorus</i>. After removing non-canonical genes (i.e., genes with premature stop codons in the coding region or wrong codon length), all-vs.-all BLASTP searches (<i>E</i>-value cutoff &lt;1<i>E</i> − 5) were performed, and clustering was conducted using the Markov cluster algorithm (MCL), with an inflation value (−<i>I</i>) of 1.5. Orthologous gene families were then analyzed using OrthoMCL (version v2.0.9)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res.13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2516">40</a></sup>. Single-copy orthologs (606 genes) were aligned using MUSCLE (version 3.8), and poorly aligned regions were trimmed using trimAI. A phylogenetic tree was constructed using the RAxML (version 8.2.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2520">41</a></sup>. The divergence time was obtained from the TimeTree database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Suleski, M. &amp; Hedges, S. B. TimeTree: a resource for timelines, timetrees, and divergence times. Mol. Biol. Evol.34, 1812–1819 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2524">42</a></sup>. Gene family expansion throughout the plant lineage was analyzed using the CAFÉ (version 4.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol.30, 1987–1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2528">43</a></sup>. Functional domains were defined by Pfam (version 27.0), and enriched domains were estimated by Fisher’s exact test (adjusted <i>p</i> value &lt;0.01) and odds ratio ≤2.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">WGBS and data analysis</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from control (CT 12 h) and MJ-treated (MJ 12 h, MJ 24 h, and MJ 48 h) <i>P. grandiflorus</i> seedlings, and libraries for WGBS were prepared as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Urich, M. A., Nery, J. R., Lister, R., Schmitz, R. J. &amp; Ecker, J. R. MethylC-seq library preparation for base-resolution whole-genome bisulfite sequencing. Nat. Protoc.10, 475–483 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2546">44</a></sup>, according to the manufacturer’s instructions (Illumina Inc., San Diego, CA, USA). The WGBS libraries were sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform, generating 100-bp paired-end reads (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#MOESM2">S16</a>). Adapters and low-quality bases were trimmed using Trimmomatic (version 0.39). The trimmed reads were mapped onto the draft genome assembly of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> using Bismark (version 0.19.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Krueger, F. &amp; Andrews, S. R. Bismark: a flexible aligner and methylation caller for Bisulfite-Seq applications. Bioinformatics27, 1571–1572 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2556">45</a></sup>, with a cutoff at ≥5 read depth. After filtering duplicate reads, the methylation status of each cytosine nucleotide was determined. The methylation data were further evaluated using the binomial test, followed by Benjamini–Hochberg false discovery rate (FDR) correction (FDR &lt; 0.01)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Hofmeister, B. T., Lee, K., Rohr, N. A., Hall, D. W. &amp; Schmitz, R. J. Stable inheritance of DNA methylation allows creation of epigenotype maps and the study of epiallele inheritance patterns in the absence of genetic variation. Genome Biol.18, 155 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0329-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d10656e2561">46</a></sup>. The methylation level was calculated using the following equation:</p><div id="Equa" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{mC/}}\left( {{\mathrm{mC + umC}}} \right)$$</span></div></div><p>where mC and umC represent the number of methylated and unmethylated reads, respectively, in three contexts (CG, CHG, and CHH).</p><p>To measure the average methylation levels in protein-coding genes, the coordinates of upstream sequences (2 kb upstream of the start codon), downstream sequences (2 kb downstream of the stop codon), and gene bodies (including exons and introns) were extracted and divided into 10, 10, and 50 bins, respectively, and the average methylation level of each bin was calculated and plotted. To identify DMCs between control and MJ treatments in protein-coding regions, sites with |ΔmC| ≥ 10 were selected; the value of |ΔmC| was calculated using the following equation:</p><div id="Equb" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\left| {{\mathrm{mC}}_{{\mathrm{Control}}\_{\mathrm{12h}}} - {\mathrm{mC}}_{{\mathrm{MJ}}\_{\mathrm{12h/24h/48h}}}} \right| \ge 10.$$</span></div></div></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Sequencing data used in this study are available in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) database under the following accession numbers: SPEA00000000 (WGS data), SRR8712510–SRR8712517 (RNA-Seq data derived from eight different tissues), SRR8712518–SRR8712529 (RNA-Seq data from MJ treatments), and SRR9005109–SRR9005120 (WGBS data from MJ treatments). In addition, the gene set of <i>P. grandiflorus</i> is available from our website <a href="http://platycodon.theragenetex.com">http://platycodon.theragenetex.com</a>.</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Bib1" data-title="References"><div class="c-article-section" id="Bib1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Bib1">References</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Bib1-content"><div data-container-section="references"><ol class="c-article-references"><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="T.
